    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f15e1b51f14a44718810a0d353b88c9b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '37871dfd-810e-4389-84d5-975b848e1db5'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d131264941" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High-voltage sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are one of the most promising energy storage technologies for abundant resources and cost-efficiency. However, their low energy density compared with lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) hinders their practical applications. The high reactivity of high-voltage cathodes, the primary factor, leads to deterioration of electrode/electrolyte interphase. Herein, a novel anion/cation solvation strategy is innovatively proposed for a ternary low-concentration electrolyte that tackles the critical bottleneck of unstable electrode/electrolyte interphase. Especially, the intermolecular interaction within as-designed electrolyte is remodeled by weakly polar fluorinated co-solvent (ethoxy(pentafluoro)cyclotriphosphazene, PFPN) compared with traditional carbonate-based electrolytes. PFPN can not only stabilize propylene carbonate (PC), reduce the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>-PC, ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, accelerating Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation, but also weaken the interaction of anions to form stable organic/inorganic composite cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI). In this work, the ionic conductivity of low-concentration electrolyte increases to 6.12 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> and the oxidation stability is successfully extended to 4.84&nbsp;V. And Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF)||Na half-cells present excellent cycling performance with average coulombic efficiency of 99.5% after 2000 cycles at 4.5&nbsp;V and NVPF||hard carbon (HC) full cells with relatively high energy density (≈450&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) competitive to commercial LIBs, which are expected to be coupled with higher voltage cathodes to achieve higher energy density in future.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315007-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315007-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent years, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gradually become one of the best potential candidates for the next generation of commercial large-scale energy storage systems, because of their abundant resources, low cost and low redox potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs.&nbsp;standard hydrogen electrode).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0001_R_d131264933e385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - SusMat Acc. Mater. Res. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, in terms of practical applications, there is still a gap between the energy density of SIBs and lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0002_R_d131264933e390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Mater. Chem. A Small Methods Small Methods Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although substantial progress has been made in the development of high-capacity and high-voltage electrode material for high energy-density SIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0003_R_d131264933e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. J. Power Sources J. Am. Chem. Soc.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the uncontrollable side reaction of the electrolyte on the cathode side at high voltage leads to the deterioration of interface stability, which is the key barrier of the development of high-performance sodium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0004_R_d131264933e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>It's worth mentioning that electrolytes play a dominant role in the electrochemical performance of the high-voltage cathode. This is mainly reflected in the fact that the decomposition of electrolyte on the cathode surface will rapidly increase as the voltage increases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0005_R_d131264933e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) derived from electrolyte decomposition is usually unstable and not dense.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0002_R_d131264933e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Mater. Chem. A Small Methods Small Methods Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0006_R_d131264933e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, this unstable CEI will lead to continuous electrolyte decomposition, low average coulombic efficiency (CE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0007_R_d131264933e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Energy Chem. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the electrode will face the dilemma of transition metal dissolution and structure degradation that eventually endow capacity decay.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0008_R_d131264933e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, exploring a new electrolyte formulation and forming a stable electrode/electrolyte interphase layer on the cathode is crucial for improving the performance of high-voltage sodium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0009_R_d131264933e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>It's well known that propylene carbonate (PC) with advantages of high boiling point (242 °C), high flash point (&gt;230 °C) and remarkable antioxidant abilities (&gt;4.8&nbsp;V, decomposition potential of pure solvent) has become the preferred choice for commercial carbonated based electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0010_R_d131264933e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a signal PC electrolyte generally has a low ionic conductivity, and the by-products produced by PC are not dense, which will fall off as cycling and cannot protect the electrode. Several strategies such as fluorinated electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0002_R_d131264933e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Mater. Chem. A Small Methods Small Methods Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0011_R_d131264933e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> high concentrated electrolytes (HCEs)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0012_R_d131264933e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and localized high concentrated electrolytes (LHCEs)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0013_R_d131264933e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been investigated to promote interface stability of high-voltage cathode in carbonate-based electrolytes. However, fluorinated electrolytes are costly and environmentally unfriendly due to the F-rich solvents even if they can form stable electrode/electrolyte interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0014_R_d131264933e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And although HCEs and form an anion-derived CEI membrane and improve the oxidation stability, the high cost, viscosity and low ionic conductivity (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0001">1a</a>) are still obstacles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0015_R_d131264933e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> None of the above strategies can meet the needs of practical applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315007-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1c4256cc-028f-41ad-8f4f-54875051e551/adfm202315007-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1c4256cc-028f-41ad-8f4f-54875051e551/adfm202315007-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/79793d8f-c8af-44d7-9f36-4cdf54982e10/adfm202315007-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1c4256cc-028f-41ad-8f4f-54875051e551/adfm202315007-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315007-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315007" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Common solvation structures and theoretical calculation of different components. a) Schematic illustration of the solvation structures with a focus on cations solvated environment. AGGs: ion aggregates, CIPs: contact ion pairs, SSIPs: solvent-separated ion pairs. b) HOMO and LUMO values of PC, PFPN, ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-solvent complexes. c) ESP of PC and PFPN molecules. d) The binding energy of different components between Na<sup>+</sup>, ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, PC and PFPN.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Very recently, weakly solvated electrolytes (WSEs)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0016_R_d131264933e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have attracted extensive attention, and provide an effective scheme for the stability of electrode/electrolyte interface on high-voltage cathode. WSEs not only express superiority in terms of cost compared with HCEs and LHCEs, but also take into account oxidation stability. Cui and coworkers prepared an ether-based WSE consisting of 1,2-diethoxyethane (DEE), a “weakly solvating power” solvent, which had more anion-rich solvation shells and enhanced interfacial stability of cathode. The full-cell with this electrolyte could realize excellent cycling performance under 4.4&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0017_R_d131264933e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Later, Wang proposed a tetrahydropyran (THP)-based WSE to regulate cation solvation structure and interfacial behaviors, which improved high-voltage stability and inhabited the dissolution of transitional metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0018_R_d131264933e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Energy Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, mostly WSEs only regulate Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure and the ionic conductivity is barely satisfactory due to the poor dissolution of sodium salts. Hence, it's highly desirable but challenging to develop a kind of electrolyte with high ionic conductivity and excellent interfacial stability for high-voltage SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we proposed a novel ternary weakly solvated low-concentration salt electrolyte for high-voltage cathodes, which is realized by anion/cation solvation strategy using a weakly polar solvent, ethoxy(pentafluoro)cyclotriphosphazene (PFPN) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0001">1a</a>). This unique design is fundamentally different from previous reports of WSEs, especially as the electrolyte can regulate the solvation structure of anions and cations simultaneously and improve the interfacial stability of high-voltage cathodes and cycling performance of sodium-ion batteries. Such a design has multiple advantages: a) PFPN can weaken the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>-PC, accelerate the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> and improve ionic conductivity. b) PFPN can reduce the interaction of PC-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, decrease PC decomposition that improves the oxidation stability and facilitate the formation of stable CEI that improves the cycling stability of the battery. c) PFPN breaks PC-PC network, stabilizes PC, and reduces the decomposition and coordination of PC. With this design, the ionic conductivity of low-concentration salt electrolyte increases to 6.12 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> and the electrochemical stability window (ESW) enlarges to 4.84&nbsp;V. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF)||Na cells exhibit an excellent average coulombic efficiency of 99.5% after 2000 cycles at 4.5&nbsp;V and NVPF||hard carbon (HC) full cells realize a relatively high energy density (≈450&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) at 4.4&nbsp;V competitive to commercial LIBs. Therefore, this work provides a promising anion/cation solvation electrolyte endowing high-voltage cathodes with durable cyclic performance for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315007-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315007-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The plausibility of PFPN as co-solvent for high-voltage SIBs was first predicted by density-functional theory (DFT) simulations. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0001">1b</a>, although the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) energy of PFPN (−8.89&nbsp;eV) is slightly lower than PC (−8.36&nbsp;eV), PFPN has excellent inherent stability and broadens the electrochemical window of PC effectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, HOMO energy levels of these ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-solvent complexes represent the opposite trend compared with Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes, and PFPN-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> exhibits lower HOMO energy, which are the major influential factor for oxidation stability. The energy levels of dimers of different solvents suggest that the intermolecular interaction between PC and PFPN improves the energy barrier of oxidation of PC and decreases the activity of PC. The charge densities of atoms in PC/PFPN solvents are performed by electrostatic potential (ESP) simulations in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0001">1c</a>. Noticeably, due to a low dielectric constant, PFPN exhibits weak charge distribution than PC, reflecting the weak solvation capacity of PFPN. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0001">1d</a>, the differences in interaction between PFPN and other components will inevitably lead to the reconstruction of cation/anion solvation structures. Moreover, the weak interactions between PFPN and PC can alleviate the electron delocalization of PC molecules, which is expected to stabilize PC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>PFPN as the co-solvent, plays the most prominent advantage when PFPN is added in NP3 electrolyte at molar ratio of PC:PFPN = 7.5:1 (donated as NPP6, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–6</a>, Supporting Information). At this concentration, the NPP6 electrolyte has the widest electrochemical window of 4.84&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, at 10&nbsp;µA cm<sup>−2</sup>) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2a</a>). And NPP6 electrolyte exhibits excellent reduction stability consistent with energy levels which can decrease the reduction decomposition of PC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, after adding PFPN, the average wetting angle of NPP6 electrolyte decreases from 15.59° (NP3) to 5.79°, indicating a significant improvement of the wetting ability of the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315007-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fc4b2667-2216-482f-84ae-d91cdc01ded5/adfm202315007-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fc4b2667-2216-482f-84ae-d91cdc01ded5/adfm202315007-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b3372507-580e-4ba7-aa62-f6f9f4dcbf43/adfm202315007-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fc4b2667-2216-482f-84ae-d91cdc01ded5/adfm202315007-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315007-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315007" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The physicochemical properties, MD simulations and solvation structures of electrolytes. a) LSV of NP3 and NPP6 with the inset wetting properties. b) The radial distribution function of Na<sup>+</sup>-PFPN in NPP6. c) O<sub>ClO4-</sub>-H<sub>solvent</sub> (dashed lines for NP3 and solid lines for NPP6). Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectra of various electrolytes d) from 2000 to 400 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra of e) <sup>17</sup>O shift from C═O in various solvents and f) <sup>19</sup>F shift from P-F of PFPN. g) Kinetics energy barrier of the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation by Arrhenius analysis. h) The solvation structure of anions.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were performed to dissect the effect of the unique electrolyte solvation structures on electrochemical stability. The radial distribution function (g(r)) of Na<sup>+</sup>-PFPN implies that it enters into the second solvated sheath through weak solvation capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2b</a>) and provides space for solvated Na<sup>+</sup> migration through steric effect, improving the ionic conductivity on the basis of diluent NP3 electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information). The reduced coordination numbers of Na<sup>+</sup> and weak interaction between PFPN and PC facilitate the formation of a loose structure based on the original solvent-separated ion pairs (SSIP, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8,S9</a>, Supporting Information), which can accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation, reducing the possibility of solvents decomposition. Besides, anion solvation structure was revealed by analyzing the interaction between O atoms of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> and H atoms from PC/PFPN solvents. PFPN, high binding energy with ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, can involve the coordination of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> and reduce ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-PC interaction to decrease PC decomposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information), thus greatly elevating oxidation stability of electrolytes. At the same time, the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> as the voltage-stimulated response species tends to rapidly move to cathode surface during charging possess. PFPN-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> complexes binding through hydrogen-like bond interaction can facilitate the formation of stable CEI and inhibit the generation of side reactions. In addition, PFPN breaks PC-PC network and stabilizes PC molecules, which reduces the activity and the possibility of decomposition and coordination of PC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information). In a word, the regulation of anion solvation structure plays a vital role on improving both oxidation stability of electrolytes and interfacial stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2h</a>), indicating PFPN as co-solvent is highly reliable for High-voltage SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was performed in order to understand the function of PFPN in the solvation structure of NPP6 electrolyte. We found that the C═O stretching frequency of free PC has a slight blueshift, from 1780 to 1783 cm<sup>−1</sup> after adding PFPN, implying that the coordination capability of PC is weaken (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2d</a> shows that the C═O stretching frequency has a redshift to 1778 cm<sup>−1</sup> in NP3 electrolyte, suggesting that the O atoms in C═O group are coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup>. There is a same trend for change of C═O frequency in NPP6 electrolyte, compared with mixed solvent. These comparative results indicate that co-solvent PFPN influences the coordination capability of PC, consistent with the MD results. Moreover, PFPN makes the strength of P-F and P-N enhanced in mixed solvent, verifying the intermolecular interaction between PC and PFPN. The blueshift of P-F, P-N stretching frequencies in NPP6 electrolyte compared with mixed solvents indicates the reconstruction of PFPN in solvation structures. PFPN can decrease the coordination capability of PC through intermolecular interactions and the unique solvation structure is expected to accelerate desolvation process on the electrode surface, reduce PC decomposition, and effectively broaden the electrochemical window of the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The different kinetics of different electrolytes, such as the energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation (E<sub>a, ct</sub>), can also reveal the solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> to some extent. These parameters can be tested and calculated based on the electrochemical impedance spectra of symmetric Na–Na cells at different temperatures (0–40 °C) through the Arrhenius equation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0019_R_d131264933e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the E<sub>a, ct</sub> of the NPP6 electrolyte is 73.27&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, which is lower than the 81.57&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in the NP3 electrolyte. These results are consistent with the spectral analysis. It shows that the intermolecular interactions in NPP6 electrolyte weaken Na<sup>+</sup>-PC interaction, and help form a loose coordination environment, which makes Na<sup>+</sup> more easily dissolvable.</p>
         
         <p>The solvation structures were further experimentally confirmed through nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy. As shown in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2e</a>, there are three sets of inequivalent <sup>17</sup>O nuclei in PC molecule, with carbonyl <sup>17</sup>O (α) between 200 and 250&nbsp;ppm, and ether <sup>17</sup>O (β and γ) between 75 and 150&nbsp;ppm. The peak position of carbonyl <sup>17</sup>O shows significant changes. We find that the <sup>17</sup>O signal is slightly downfield shifted when PFPN is added into pure PC solvent, demonstrating the de-shielding process (decrease in electron density) around the O nuclei (C═O) of PC, consistent with charge density difference (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information). This means that PFPN can optimize the electron cloud density of the O nuclei of the C═O functional group by adjusting intermolecular interactions. In addition, the upshift of <sup>17</sup>O (C═O) from PC when salt is added in NP3 and NPP6 electrolytes is ascribed to the shielding effect (increase in electron cloud density) of PC that participated in the inner solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup>. The same downshift trend is also found in NPP6 electrolyte compared with baseline electrolyte, which demonstrates that PFPN weakens the coordination capability of PC, combined with FTIR results. More evidence can be found in the <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra (P-F) of PFPN in different solvent mixtures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0002">2f</a>). Compared to pure PFPN solvent, a downfield shift of the <sup>19</sup>F signal (P-F) is observed in the mixed solvent. This indicates a decrease in electron cloud density around the F nuclei and reconfirms the intermolecular interactions of PC-PFPN, which proves the changes in the electronic structure of PFPN, consistent with the FTIR test. There is a slightly upshift in NPP6 electrolyte after introducing NaClO<sub>4</sub>, which proves the weakly coordination capability of PFPN-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> in NPP6 electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b,c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the high-voltage stability of various electrolytes, NVPF||Na half-cells were tested with charging voltage of 4.3&nbsp;V. Using the NP3 electrolyte as a blank reference, the improvement of the electrochemical performance of the NVPF||Na cells with NPP6 electrolyte is apparent. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0003">3a</a> exhibits the first charge/discharge curves of NVPF||Na half-cells at the current density of 0.5 C (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) in different electrolytes. The initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of NPP6 electrolyte reaches 85.0%, while that of NP3 electrolyte is only 72.3%. This is related to the narrower electrochemical window and more severe side reactions with NP3 electrolyte. The reversibility of the charge/discharge curves at low current densities gradually improves with the first three cycles in NPP6 electrolyte, and the non-overlapping charge/discharge curves of the first few cycles may be related to the formation of an interfacial layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information). However, the baseline electrolyte always maintains a low level of coulombic efficiency (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the NVPF||Na cells with NPP6 electrolyte also display excellent rate performance with reversible capacities of 104.5 and 85.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C and 10 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0003">3b</a>), respectively, which is attributed to higher ionic conductivity, faster desolvaion kinetics. This may be related to continuous decomposition of solvents, deterioration of sodium salt dissociation and decrease of solution conductivity during the cycling process in NP3 electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315007-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62dbd9fa-6798-494c-bfd2-27e1dd004d8e/adfm202315007-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/62dbd9fa-6798-494c-bfd2-27e1dd004d8e/adfm202315007-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/066974af-9f25-4f63-bf73-e13cb4a763c6/adfm202315007-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/62dbd9fa-6798-494c-bfd2-27e1dd004d8e/adfm202315007-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315007-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315007" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performances. a) Initial charge/discharge curves, b) rate performance, c,d) cycling performance at 1 C and 5 C, respectively of NVPF||Na with different electrolytes. e,f) the charge/discharge curves of different cycles of NVPF||Na with NPP6 and NP3 at 5 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Long-term cycling tests can directly reflect the improvement of cycling stability. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0003">3c</a> demonstrates that these NVPF||Na cells with the NPP6 electrolyte have superior cycling stability. At 1 C, the NVPF||Na cell with blank electrolyte exhibits low-capacity retention of 52.1% with low ICE of 72.8% and average CE of 97.5% after 200 cycles. Remarkably, these cells with NPP6 electrolyte could achieve capacity retention of 80.5% after 400 cycles with ICE of 86.8% and average CE of 98.8%, which indicates superior reversibility for electrode reaction owing to the formation of the stable CEI in NPP6 electrolyte. In addition, the cycling stability of NVPF||Na half-cells at higher current density was also tested. Consistent with the above results, these cells using the NP3 electrolyte fail in a shorter time, with capacity retention of &lt;30% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0003">3d</a>). In order to exclude the influence of Na anodes in the PC-based electrolyte, we coupled the cycled NVPF cathode in NP3 electrolyte after 300 cycles with a fresh Na anode. The newly assembled NVPF||Na cell has the initial capacity of only 58.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and also undergoes rapid decay, which demonstrates the important role of CEI in cycling stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). These NVPF||Na cells at higher cut-off voltage of 4.5&nbsp;V were tested to investigate the practicality of NPP6 electrolyte at higher voltage. These cells also achieved excellent cycling stability with high ICE of 84.2%/87.1% and average CE over 98.5%/99.5% at 1 C and 5 C, respectively (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15,S16</a>, Supporting Information). This is significantly better than those performances of blank electrolyte. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0003">3e,f</a> are the charge/discharge curves of cells in different electrolytes at 5 C, and the cell using the NP3 electrolyte exhibits a change in the charge/discharge behavior with the increase of cycle numbers, possibly because the battery could not form dense and stable CEI film during the cycling process and maintain the stability of cathode. The high-valent V<sup>4+</sup> dissolves during cycling and causes capacity decay. The above results prove superior anti-oxidation stability and superior compatibility with high-voltage NVPF cathode for NPP6 electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), another vanadium-based common cathode material, has high specific capacity, excellent rate performance and long cycling life. NVP||Na cells were tested with charge/discharge range of 2.5–3.8&nbsp;V. The NVP||Na cells with NP3 electrolyte display low-capacity retention of 5.25% after 1000 cycles, while the capacity of NVP||Na cells with NPP6 electrolyte is almost no attenuation with superior capacity retention of 100% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The same decay phenomenon indicates that the high-charged V<sup>4+</sup> dissolves, leading to irreversible charge/discharge behavior of the cell, and ultimately resulting to capacity decay, verifying the above conjecture.</p>
         
         <p>More intuitively, it is found that the membrane cycled in NP3 electrolyte becomes yellow, and the vanadium content at the residual electrolyte of fiber separator is as high as 335&nbsp;ppm (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0004">4a</a>). The dissolved vanadium suggests severe cathode decay. In the identical situation, the vanadium content in NPP6 electrolyte is just &lt;5% of that in NP3 electrolyte, while no obvious color change is observed in cycled membrane. The significantly decreased vanadium dissolution in NPP6 electrolyte demonstrates that NVPF cathode degradation is greatly reduced. The high binding energy of PC to V<sup>4+</sup> indicates that the lack of stable CEI in NP3 electrolyte may cause excessive dissolution of the transitional metal (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0004">4b</a>). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0004">4c</a>, the cycled cathode in NPP6 electrolyte shows visible differences in peak intensity. The reduction in peak number and peak intensity both indicate the collapse and failure of the crystal structure of cathode material, resulting in battery failure. Constant-voltage floating tests of NVPF||Na cells at 4.3&nbsp;V were conducted to evaluate the parasitic reactions between cathode and electrolyte. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0004">4d</a> implies that NPP6 electrolyte greatly reduces the leakage current during 4.3&nbsp;V floating tests, implying reduced electrolyte decomposition and improved oxidation stability in NPP6 electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315007-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fbe1761c-cc1f-4a0b-bc9e-c8f84fa22aca/adfm202315007-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fbe1761c-cc1f-4a0b-bc9e-c8f84fa22aca/adfm202315007-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f1a3a21a-b2f9-466c-afdb-a97ca2dd51a7/adfm202315007-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fbe1761c-cc1f-4a0b-bc9e-c8f84fa22aca/adfm202315007-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315007-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315007" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The analysis of electrode-electrolyte interphase. a) Elemental analysis of dissolved vanadium ions in different electrolytes after 200 cycles (inset are photos of the GF membranes). b) Binding energy between PC, PFPN and V<sup>4+</sup>. c) the XRD of cycled NVPF cathode in different electrolytes and raw NVPF material. d) Static discharging curves of NVPF||Na charged to 4.3&nbsp;V for 24 h. e) EIS and f) Warburg coefficient of NVPF||Na half-cells after 100 cycles. g) Schematic diagram of different electrolytes on the NVPF surface.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Moreover, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) spectra of cycled NVPF||Na cells also provide powerful evidences. The semicircle region in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0004">4e</a> corresponds to the charge transfer impedance of NVPF||Na half-cells, indicating that the charge transfer impedance of the battery circulating in the NP3 electrolyte is much larger than that of the NPP6 electrolyte. The Warburg diffusion coefficient in NP3 electrolyte is also bigger than that of NPP6 electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0004">4f</a>), which illustrates that the D<sub>Na+</sub> in NPP6 electrolyte is bigger to the benefit of superior rate performance. In a word, the side reactions in blank electrolyte are more severe, resulting to worse rate performance and cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>The outstanding electrochemical stability of the NPP6 electrolyte has been demonstrated from the electrolyte, while the CEI, the other important factor, has been further studied through specific morphology and composition analysis. The TEM image shows that the thickness of the heterogeneous CEI in the blank electrolyte is 2–11&nbsp;nm (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0005">5b</a>). The formation of thick and uneven CEI may be due to severe decomposition of the electrolyte, resulting in low CE and poor cycling stability. After PFPN is introduced, the CEI film is significantly improved, and the complete CEI with a thickness of ≈3&nbsp;nm is observed, which should be one of the important reasons for the improvement of the battery performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0005">5a</a>). In addition, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was also conducted to determine the chemical composition of the CEI formed in different electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). The CEI film formed in the NPP6 electrolyte belongs to the organic/inorganic composite CEI, including C-C, C-O, C═O, P-F, P-N, and NO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0020_R_d131264933e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the NPP6 electrolyte, the CEI formed the blank electrolyte has a higher O atom ratio, which further verifies a higher degree of electrolyte decomposition in NP3 electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). P-N and NO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> species are observed in the CEI of the NPP6 electrolyte, indicating that PFPN participated in the formation CEI. It is precisely because PFPN decomposes and generates uniform and stable organic/inorganic composite CEI on the cathode surface that the cycling performance of the NVPF||Na battery is significantly improved.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315007-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19ed5444-31a3-4468-a324-ab2590a021e8/adfm202315007-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/19ed5444-31a3-4468-a324-ab2590a021e8/adfm202315007-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6a7699f7-4d16-4415-9c84-d113bc98cdbc/adfm202315007-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19ed5444-31a3-4468-a324-ab2590a021e8/adfm202315007-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315007-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315007" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) TEM of cycled NVPF cathode in NPP6 and NP3 respectively. c,d) P 2p and N 1s profiles for NVPF cathode after ten cycles in NPP6 and NP3 electrolytes. e,f) The DRT of different electrolytes (Inset: EIS of different cycles). g–i) The first three cycles charge/discharge curves, rate performance and cycling performance of NVPF||HC full battery at 4.3&nbsp;V in NPP6 electrolyte.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The evolution process of cathode electrolyte interphase can be roughly observed by EIS analysis. However, many different physicochemical processes or a complex process with different steps often have similar spectral characteristics in EIS. The distribution of relaxation time (DRT) is an analysis method based on impedance spectra that can distinguish different steps of complex chemical processes and analyze different reaction processes in battery systems corresponding to different relaxation times, such as ion transfer, charge transfer, and electron diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0021_R_d131264933e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochim. Acta">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We also introduced this technique to analyze the decomposition side reactions of NVPF||Na cells after different cycles in different electrolytes. According to the test results, there are four regions in the DRT spectrum, respectively corresponding to different physicochemical processes: ion transfer in bulk electrolyte, cathode-electrolyte interface, charge transfer in solid-state interphase, and solid-state diffusion in electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0005">5e,f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315007-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315007-bib-0022_R_d131264933e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Joule">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combined with the DRT analysis of the symmetric Na||Na cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), it can be determined that the process corresponding to the relaxation time in the range of 10<sup>−4</sup>–10<sup>−2</sup> s is the formation process of CEI film. With the increase of the cycle number, the impedance of CEI film in the NP3 electrolyte changes rapidly and increases more, while the impedance value in the NPP6 electrolyte is maintained at a stable level. This is closely to the continuous decomposition of solvent molecules in the blank electrolyte, while the stable CEI derived from the unique solvation structure in the NPP6 electrolyte effectively prevents the decomposition side reactions of the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The practical application prospect of NPP6 electrolyte was estimated by full cells with commercial hard carbon (HC), denoted as NVPF||HC. Before assembling the full cell, the HC is run in the half cell for two cycles to calibrate its capacity and is then discharged to 0.1&nbsp;V to complete a pre-sodiation process. The NVPF||HC full cell delivers an impressive specific capacity of 114.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0005">5g</a>). Additionally, the NVPF||HC full cell exhibits a competitive rate performance and cycling performance, rendering the capacity retention of 87.4% after 200 cycles at 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315007-fig-0005">5h,i</a>). And the cell realizes a relatively high energy density (≈450&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, based on NVPF mass) at higher charging voltage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), which displays a wide prospect in the large-scale energy storage field.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315007-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315007-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we successfully designed a novel ternary weakly solvated low-concentration salt electrolyte with a wide electrochemical window by the anion/cation solvent regulation strategy. By introducing weakly soluble PFPN as a co-solvent in the low-concentration salt electrolyte, the reconstruction of the solvation structure of anion/cation was realized. PFPN appears in the second solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup>, provides space for solvated Na<sup>+</sup> migration, reduces the coordination number of Na<sup>+</sup> with PC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, and separates PC from Na<sup>+</sup>, accelerating the desolvation kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> and enhancing the antioxidant stability of the electrolyte. At the same time, PFPN weakens the interaction between ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-solvent complexes and decreases the decomposition of PC. Under the drive of the electric field, it can decompose to form stable and uniform high-quality CEI on the cathode surface, enhancing the cycling stability of the battery. The intermolecular interaction between PC and PFPN can stabilize PC and decrease the possibility of decomposition and coordination. Combined with these characteristics, the NVPF||Na battery with this electrolyte has good ionic conductivity (6.12 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>), antioxidation ability (4.84&nbsp;V), excellent cycling stability (2000 cycles) and energy density (≈450&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). This study provides a feasible strategy for developing low-concentration electrolytes suitable for high-voltage cathodes to achieve higher energy density.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315007-sec-0040-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
